    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14219446110796731184">In <b>Theatre</b>, W. Somerset Maugham–the author of the classic novels <b>Of Human Bondage</b> and <b>Up at the Villa</b>–introduces us to Julia Lambert, a woman of breathtaking poise and talent whose looks have stood by her forty-six years. She is a star stage actress England–so good, in fact, that perhaps she never stops acting.<br/><br/>It seems that noting can ruffle her satin feathers, until a qui</span>
  <span id="freeText14219446110796731184" style="display:none">In <b>Theatre</b>, W. Somerset Maugham–the author of the classic novels <b>Of Human Bondage</b> and <b>Up at the Villa</b>–introduces us to Julia Lambert, a woman of breathtaking poise and talent whose looks have stood by her forty-six years. She is a star stage actress England–so good, in fact, that perhaps she never stops acting.<br /><br />It seems that noting can ruffle her satin feathers, until a quiet stranger who challenges Julia's very sense of self. As a result, she will endure rejection for the first time, her capacity as a mother will be affronted, and her ability to put on whatever face she desired for her public will prove limited. In <b>Theatre</b>, Maugham subtly exposes the tensions and triumphs that occur when acting and reality blend together, and–for Julia–ultimately reverse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14219446110796731184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1807859064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1807859064" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1807859064?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fav-authors">fav-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=vintage">vintage</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1807859064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9037840345380561172"><i>
  <blockquote>But where are you? If one stripped you of your exhibitionism, if one took your technique away from you, if one peeled you as one peels an onion of skin after skin of pretence and insincerity, of tags of old parts and shreds of faked emotions, would one come upon a soul at last?</blockquote>
</i><br/>Oh Julia, you who live on a stage and we live there with you and your fleeting love (lovers); your complicated, yet undisturbed marriage; your disdain of boredom; your glorious moments in the limelight. All the world r</span>
  <span id="freeText9037840345380561172" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>But where are you? If one stripped you of your exhibitionism, if one took your technique away from you, if one peeled you as one peels an onion of skin after skin of pretence and insincerity, of tags of old parts and shreds of faked emotions, would one come upon a soul at last?</blockquote>
</i><br />Oh Julia, you who live on a stage and we live there with you and your fleeting love (lovers); your complicated, yet undisturbed marriage; your disdain of boredom; your glorious moments in the limelight. All the world revolves around you and we only partake. If life is indeed a stage, some are better actresses. <br /><br />How do you see yourself when you don't have a title that the job or society gives you? Who are you without them? Do you like what you see at night, in the mirror? Do you love life or desire something more of it? There are many more questions this book asks, but most importantly, it lays bare the theme of desire and the toll desire takes on the human mind. I haven't seen the movie to this book (<i>Being Julia</i>), but I've heard from someone who saw me reading this, that Lord Charles is said to be Julia's lover in the movie? Well, with an emphatic, bold statement that excludes any spoiler tags, I'll say this: no he was not, not in the true sense of the word. Sorry, I've gone and said it, for I'm usually perturbed when a movie tries to veer away from the facts of the novel on which it is written. But I'll get off my anal horse now. (Note to self: must see movie).<br /><br />I loved the references to Shakespeare and Keats, the explorations of tension and conflict that occurred alongside this giant theatre. Lust is the leading woman and love her gentleman. Everyone else sees themselves through her. This is my third Maugham and my least favorite so far, although I can see most readers liking this better than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/191793.Cakes_and_Ale" title="Cakes and Ale by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Cakes and Ale</a>. The prose I didn't find affecting as I usually find with Maugham's careful prose, and yet the story is a bit appealing, with a female protagonist empowered by her work. The novel leans on dialogue and inner thought that occurs through white spaces of dialogue, but it almost feels bare, as if one is reading a play that was filled into a novel. Some of the intricacies are missing and although Julia has layers, there is a certain quality missing in her experiences. But this is a book you read for its exploring moments: of reality and illusion, of self and perceptions of self.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9037840345380561172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4325641521584316032">Middle-brows like to put down Maugham, but smarties know you can't put him down. As the author of hit plays, he knew Ethel Barrymore, Gladys Cooper, among others. The stage is an effective prop as he warmly scopes the psyche of a plummy star (her set: asexual husby and priggy son, lesby backer, shopboy lover and his thespy sweetie -- ah, theatuuh). A sublime 'recognition' scene missing from 2 film versions: bored to hell, star decides to 'give' herself to Devoted Friend. Imagine her shock when s</span>
  <span id="freeText4325641521584316032" style="display:none">Middle-brows like to put down Maugham, but smarties know you can't put him down. As the author of hit plays, he knew Ethel Barrymore, Gladys Cooper, among others. The stage is an effective prop as he warmly scopes the psyche of a plummy star (her set: asexual husby and priggy son, lesby backer, shopboy lover and his thespy sweetie -- ah, theatuuh). A sublime 'recognition' scene missing from 2 film versions: bored to hell, star decides to 'give' herself to Devoted Friend. Imagine her shock when she realizes he isn't interested in sex, probably was <i> never </i> interested.<br /><br />The finale is also cherce. After whammy premiere, she flees by herself to a restaurant and celebrates with verboten steak and fried potatoes. What greater pleasure, at times, can top being left alone? Maugham writes from the bowels of compassion.</span>

<div id="review_3423777963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3423777963?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikey B." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikey B." href="/user/show/1484029-mikey-b">Mikey B.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099286837" href="/book/show/1077363.Theatre">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1484029-mikey-b?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3423777963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3247465158321138989">Like all of Maugham’s novels there is always a clarity in this story about a famous actress (Julia) on the British stage. Maugham, also being a famous playwright, was fully acquainted with the life of the theatre and the lives of the actors, stage managers and the like. All the characters are very well delineated with snappy dialogue.<br/><br/>All that being said, this is not among the top-ranked of Maugham’s works. There is a soap opera feel to Julia’s repeated crisis with her younger lover. It becomes o</span>
  <span id="freeText3247465158321138989" style="display:none">Like all of Maugham’s novels there is always a clarity in this story about a famous actress (Julia) on the British stage. Maugham, also being a famous playwright, was fully acquainted with the life of the theatre and the lives of the actors, stage managers and the like. All the characters are very well delineated with snappy dialogue.<br /><br />All that being said, this is not among the top-ranked of Maugham’s works. There is a soap opera feel to Julia’s repeated crisis with her younger lover. It becomes overwrought and the vanity exhibited by all the characters becomes oppressive. In contrast to many of Maugham’s other works (like those about British colonists in the South Seas) this story has a restrictive and confined feeling, hardly ever going outside of itself.<br /><br />It was a welcome relief in the latter portion of the book when Julia journeyed to France. In chapter 27 (there are 29 chapters) Julia has a confrontation with her son which provides the most profound scene in the entire book. After that we are taken back to the theatre and it all seems anti-climatic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3247465158321138989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_452428133" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/452428133" />
    <a title="Mikela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4405952-mikela"><img alt="Mikela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398731306p2/4405952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/452428133?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikela" href="/user/show/4405952-mikela">Mikela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=1900s">1900s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=books-read-in-2013">books-read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer452428133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12210809020081299055">My first introduction to Maugham's work many years ago was with Of Human Bondage and I was so moved by this story of sexual obsession that although the details are fuzzy in my memory, the emotions evoked are still fresh in my mind. I think the ending had me crying for days. In a different way, The Razor's Edge proved itself to be a worthy classic and cemented in my opinion that Maugham is a supreme writer capable of bringing his characters to life with all the pain and passion that love can brin</span>
  <span id="freeText12210809020081299055" style="display:none">My first introduction to Maugham's work many years ago was with Of Human Bondage and I was so moved by this story of sexual obsession that although the details are fuzzy in my memory, the emotions evoked are still fresh in my mind. I think the ending had me crying for days. In a different way, The Razor's Edge proved itself to be a worthy classic and cemented in my opinion that Maugham is a supreme writer capable of bringing his characters to life with all the pain and passion that love can bring. I've read many times that the critics were not kind to his work citing his plain prose style and lack of a large vocabulary among some of his many literary failings. The reading public, however appreciated his work and to this day his books are still widely read and loved.<br /><br />Theatre introduces us to Julia Lambert, the reigning queen of London stage who at the age of forty-six, after decades of always playing a part both on and off stage,starts to doubt herself as a person. Although adored by the theatre going public, she's been in a passionless yet highly successful marriage to a part-time actor, full-time theatre manager, Michael. To her one child, Roger, she's a distant mother who found the idea of having a child much more appealing than the reality of raising one. After years of being virtuously faithful in her marriage, Julia finds herself totally enamored with a young man who sees his path to the society that he wishes to enter obtainable through her. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Despite the difference in their ages, his attention is flattering and they begin an affair, so passionate that Julia finds herself looking and feeling 20 years younger until the time he rejects her, leaving Julia hurt, humiliated and at a loss. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Is her sex appeal only on the stage? Can she be attractive to men as herself without the trappings of stage craft? Is she only bits and pieces of all the roles she has acted?<br /><br />We are taken from the early days of the courtship between Michael and Julia, the beginnings of their long careers, their many successes and through Julia's eyes we see her act her way through life, her every studied gesture, every situation that arises becoming an opportunity to play a role, how real life and the make-believe world of the theatre are so intertwined as to become one and the same - reality blurred. Maugham took us behind the curtain, stripped away the glamour and revealed all the hard work and rehearsals required to be "natural" on stage.<br /><br />I absolutely loved Julia with her total self absorption, her husband, son, friends, fellow cast members, and lover being only bit players to the grand act that she called her life. While not really connecting with others, she was never a snob and on the whole kind, loyal and generous to others but also capable of savage yet subtle revenge on those who she believes have wronged her. I loved this book, finding it impossible to put down, even re-reading several parts of it and would very happily have continued reading for another hundred or so pages...I didn't want it to end.! This book is one that is very easy to recommend to others who are interested in good literature.<br /><br />Originally published on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.chapterofdreams.com">www.chapterofdreams.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12210809020081299055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2312630842" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2312630842" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2312630842?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=great-britain">great-britain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=arts">arts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2312630842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6866829894047860894">In the preface to this book and in the audiobook, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4176632.W__Somerset_Maugham' title='W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>W. Somerset Maugham</a> tells us that over the thirty years between the production of his first play and his last he came to know “a great number of distinguished actresses. Julia Lambert, the heroine of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31326.Theatre' title='Theatre by W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>Theatre</a>, is a portrait of none of them. I have taken a trait here and a trait there and sought to create a living person. Because I was not much affected by the glamour of the brilliant creatures I had known in the flesh I drew the creature of my fan</span>
  <span id="freeText6866829894047860894" style="display:none">In the preface to this book and in the audiobook, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4176632.W__Somerset_Maugham" title="W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">W. Somerset Maugham</a> tells us that over the thirty years between the production of his first play and his last he came to know “a great number of distinguished actresses. Julia Lambert, the heroine of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31326.Theatre" title="Theatre by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Theatre</a>, is a portrait of none of them. I have taken a trait here and a trait there and sought to create a living person. Because I was not much affected by the glamour of the brilliant creatures I had known in the flesh I drew the creature of my fancy, I dare say, with a certain coolness. I think Julia is true to life.... I feel a great affection for her; I am not shocked by her naughtiness, not scandalized by her absurdities.”<br /><br />This quote tells us the author’s intentions and hints at what lies in store. Julia, the novel’s central protagonist, is the author’s creation, a composite figure of many. In my view, by taking traits from an assortment of different people, Julia fails to become real, fails to become the living person Maugham sought to create. We are given a figure made up of a conglomeration of traits found in successful actresses, but not a creature that lives and breathes. I did not come to understand her choices or why she did what she did. Her relationships with her husband, son and lovers did not ring true to me. She loves her husband and then she doesn’t, but we never come to understand why her perception of him has so radically changed. She is an actress, only an actress albeit in all that she says, thinks and does, but not a full person.<br /> <br />The book focuses on drawing a “born actress” rather than drawing one realistically drawn woman who happens to be a successful actress. Do you see the difference? Am I clear? The book <b>does</b> ask:<br />What makes an actor or actress tick?<br />What drives them?<br />How do they see themselves?<br />How do they react to the world around them? <br /><u>And can a born actor or actress ever <b>stop</b> acting</u>? <br /><br />Julia’s son Roger says to his mother:<br />“You don't know the difference between truth and make-believe. You never stop acting. It's second nature to you. You act when there's a party here. You act to the servants, you act to father, you act to me. To me you act the part of the fond, indulgent, celebrated mother. You don't exist, you're only the innumerable parts you've played. I've often wondered if there was ever a you or if you were never anything more than a vehicle for all these other people that you've pretended to be. When I've seen you go into an empty room I've sometimes wanted to open the door suddenly, but I've been afraid to in case I found nobody there.” <br /><br />Look at the quote in the preface for a second time. Maugham tells us that he drew Julia with a “certain coolness”. This coolness, this detachment, is evident in the writing. I didn’t like this.<br /><br />The setting of the book is primarily England before and after the First World War. There are excursions to France.<br /><br />The audiobook is well narrated by Lucy Scott, although the preface is read way too fast, like she was saying to us, “Let’s just skip this and get to the story!” Afterwards, the narration is easier to follow. She captures different accents well.<br /><br />This is certainly not my favorite book by the author, but I do not regret reading it. It’s OK. I know little about the personality traits of actors and actresses. As a playwright, Maugham did. In this respect I found it mildly interesting to hear what he had to say about those in the acting profession. I cannot say I am attracted by such people. Viewed simply as a book about a fictional character, I was not sufficiently drawn in.<br /><br />My ratings of Maugham’s books:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3616323.Mrs_Craddock" title="Mrs Craddock by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Mrs Craddock</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/191793.Cakes_and_Ale" title="Cakes and Ale by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Cakes and Ale</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99664.The_Painted_Veil" title="The Painted Veil by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Veil</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31196.The_Razor_s_Edge" title="The Razor's Edge by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Razor's Edge</a> 3 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397539.Christmas_Holiday" title="Christmas Holiday by W. <div id="review_796519008" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/796519008" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/796519008?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099286837" href="/book/show/1077363.Theatre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer796519008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer800158080071888163">This wonderful novel was published in 1937, when W. Somerset Maugham was already an extremely successful writer – both as a novelist and in the theatre. In fact, much of Maugham’s early success was in the theatre and it was a world he knew extremely well. <br/><br/>The heroine of this book is Julia Lambert, an ageing actress whose husband, Michael Gosselyn, manages the Siddons Theatre and her career. They have one son, Roger, and a marriage which is respectable, but passionless. Julia is flattered when he</span>
  <span id="freeText800158080071888163" style="display:none">This wonderful novel was published in 1937, when W. Somerset Maugham was already an extremely successful writer – both as a novelist and in the theatre. In fact, much of Maugham’s early success was in the theatre and it was a world he knew extremely well. <br /><br />The heroine of this book is Julia Lambert, an ageing actress whose husband, Michael Gosselyn, manages the Siddons Theatre and her career. They have one son, Roger, and a marriage which is respectable, but passionless. Julia is flattered when her husband brings a young man to lunch; an accountant who obviously admires her. Neither Julia, nor Michael, even know the young man’s name, but she sets out to be charming. Julia is certainly not without admirers, including the wealthy Charles Tamerley, but she uses her abilities as an actress to control and manipulate men. However, the young man in question, Tom Fennell, turns out to be a surprise – as before long she becomes besotted with him. <br /><br />Maugham writes of the situation that Julia finds herself in with exquisite sensitivity. You feel for her, as events spiral out of her control – and as for the ending.... Well, I have no wish to give anything away, but it is brilliantly written. This is Maugham at his most self assured and confident, in his prime and writing of a world he loved and understood. It is one of his lesser known novels, which is a shame, as it is an utter joy to read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="800158080071888163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39170571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7862046656139551223">It's funny how life works out -- I was at the local library, trying to find a book to cheer myself up when lo, I encounter the one author that has never failed me. Like all the other Maugham novels, I had no trouble with the style, voice, plot, characters, or reflections of this story. I don't agree with his take on love, but I understand what he means. I love that reading Maugham's novels is like reading something that I would write for myself. (Somerset Maugham; 300)</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9962713705591516605">It doesn't matter whether Maugham is dealing with London society or life on a small Pacific island, his characters come to life and what matters to them matters to me.   <span id="freeText9962713705591516605" style="display:none">It doesn't matter whether Maugham is dealing with London society or life on a small Pacific island, his characters come to life and what matters to them matters to me. That's what I enjoy in his novels and what I would like to learn from him.<br /><br />I believe that clothes do not make a man, nor does a plot make a novel. All depends on the characters, how they see and hear and think and feel, not what they do, but how they do it, who they are.<br /><br />In this novel, he puts the reader inside the mind of an actress, with insights into the art of acting, as well as musings on the relation between make-believe and reality.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1401926230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1401926230" />
    <a title="C.S. Burrough" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29928450-c-s-burrough"><img alt="C.S. Burrough" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442982800p2/29928450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1401926230?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C.S. Burrough" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C.S. Burrough" href="/user/show/29928450-c-s-burrough">C.S. Burrough</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1401926230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12916626894432579749">After relishing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage' title='Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>Of Human Bondage</a>, penned 22 years before this and adapted into a career-defining Bette Davis movie, I was surprised on several levels by <i>Theatre</i>, whose 2004 screen adaption scored Annette Bening a Best Actress Golden Globe and an Oscar nomination for <i>Being Julia</i>.<br/><br/>Firstly, I was surprised by its great readability, of the kind that defies conventional analysis; that literary X-factor distinguishing great writers from good ones, their material striking an artful balance between adequ</span>
  <span id="freeText12916626894432579749" style="display:none">After relishing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a>, penned 22 years before this and adapted into a career-defining Bette Davis movie, I was surprised on several levels by <i>Theatre</i>, whose 2004 screen adaption scored Annette Bening a Best Actress Golden Globe and an Oscar nomination for <i>Being Julia</i>.<br /><br />Firstly, I was surprised by its great readability, of the kind that defies conventional analysis; that literary X-factor distinguishing great writers from good ones, their material striking an artful balance between adequacy and audacity. <br /><br />Secondly, I was surprised that <i>Theatre</i>'s magic is not in its delivery, which is clunky for such a successful wordsmith (he had this published in 1937, forty years after his breakthrough novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/398936.Liza_of_Lambeth" title="Liza of Lambeth by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Liza of Lambeth</a>). Nor is his command of vocabulary so apparent here, as was noted by contemporary critics, several of whom were unimpressed by this novel.<br /><br />Thirdly, I was surprised to see that word economy was not one of <i>Theatre</i>'s notable stylistic features. Maugham's evolved indifference to narrative refinement suggests publication teams had become shy of engaging with this giant. Nor is the style, conversely, so flamboyant. <br /><br />Perhaps he had simply come to hold less concern for form than his less prolific contemporaries, more confidence in the purity of his storytelling. This is strangely reassuring. <br /><br />Those first three questions collectively begged the fourth and ultimate one for me: how did he get away with being so blasé?<br /><br />I believe the answer is that, like so many prolific masters of the era, Maugham had relaxed into his art sufficiently not to need to prove much anymore. This piece might never have kick started his career, decades before; his vast readership had simply, by 1937, developed a steady appetite for whatever he wrote.<br /><br />The essence of this fiction lies in its bare substance, rather than its presentation. As such, <i>Theatre</i> defies the discerning reader's better judgement by refusing to be put down despite conspicuous imperfections. Its key strength lies in the authentic characterisation, most notably that of protagonist Julia Lambert. <br /><br />Perhaps a crucial ingredient is its triggering of the reader's speculation as to which of this novelist-playwright's countless actress friends Julia Lambert parodies – not that she is a mere parody; on the contrary, here is a finely nuanced and compellingly original heroine. Maugham was famously friends with the likes of Gladys Cooper and Ethel Barrymore, to name but a couple, which lures the inquisitive mind down intriguing paths.<br /><br />I devoured this roughly crafted gem like a famished hyena and shan't hesitate rereading it down the track.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12916626894432579749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_785101819" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/785101819" />
    <a title="Shamim E. Haque " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5398524-shamim-e-haque"><img alt="Shamim E. Haque " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304837406p2/5398524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/785101819?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shamim E. Haque " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shamim E. Haque " href="/user/show/5398524-shamim-e-haque">Shamim E. Haque </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099286837" href="/book/show/1077363.Theatre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer785101819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16865919060125136150">W. Somerset Maugham had written novels that are far more interesting and thought provoking than this one. But this novel is not uninteresting and those who appreciate Maugham will find Theatre entertaining. Maugham's experience as a professional playwright and his intimate knowledge of the world of theatre is certainly utilized to great effect. But this novel is not so much about the world of theatre as it is about the angst of a very successful woman who loses control over herself as she finds </span>
  <span id="freeText16865919060125136150" style="display:none">W. Somerset Maugham had written novels that are far more interesting and thought provoking than this one. But this novel is not uninteresting and those who appreciate Maugham will find Theatre entertaining. Maugham's experience as a professional playwright and his intimate knowledge of the world of theatre is certainly utilized to great effect. But this novel is not so much about the world of theatre as it is about the angst of a very successful woman who loses control over herself as she finds her absorbed in love with a man much younger than herself. The plot is a rather simple three step tale of the middle aged protagonist falling in love, then being passed over for a younger and attractive contender, and the final denouement in which the protagonist comes to terms with the end of the affair and falls out of love. Maugham's narrative power and matter of fact style is first rate, and that is what makes the story so enjoyable and interesting. I found the last few chapters rather instructive. Maugham ridicules the transitory nature of love and somehow brings home the truth that 'love' will inevitably leave us unruffled, dejected and rob us of our self-esteem. Even strong and worldly characters like Julia Lambert will not find it easy to bounce back! Particularly enjoyable is the last chapter in which Julia-normally so conscious of her figure and careful with food- gives in to the temptation of relishing all by herself a dish of fried onions and gourmet beef steak. As she eats, she appreciates the unmitigated pleasure this abandonment accords her. Her final moment of regaining control and relegating to the past all her cares about Tom (her object of affection) is neither vicious nor critical. This portrayal is tongue in cheek and without any cynical touch. The pleasure of eating and drinking helps her reflect without acrimony that love cannot and will not last, yet happiness maybe redeemed in a more surefooted manner while being ensconced in a quiet corner of a fancy London restaurant presiding over succulent food. In a sense Theatre is a very urbane comedy which does not read like one owing to its author's well chosen words and parsimonious style. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16865919060125136150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117574002" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15832116120514111165">"When I've seen you go into an empty room I've sometimes wanted to open the door suddenly, but I've been afraid to in case I found nobody there."<br/><br/>That's what Julia Lambert's teenage son tells her. The greatest actress on the London stage, but fast approaching late middle age, her entire life is one grand performance, and she seems to always know what the role calls for. She's the adoring, faithful wife in a sexless marriage to Michael, her stage manager/director husband, who's a former actor of m</span>
  <span id="freeText15832116120514111165" style="display:none">"When I've seen you go into an empty room I've sometimes wanted to open the door suddenly, but I've been afraid to in case I found nobody there."<br /><br />That's what Julia Lambert's teenage son tells her. The greatest actress on the London stage, but fast approaching late middle age, her entire life is one grand performance, and she seems to always know what the role calls for. She's the adoring, faithful wife in a sexless marriage to Michael, her stage manager/director husband, who's a former actor of mild talent and extraordinary beauty. She manages, mostly, to conceal her true feelings for Tom, the oversexed, social-climbing, poor and very young accountant with whom she has her first and only serious affair. She makes many dramatic scenes for Charles, the older wealthy man who's loved her for twenty years--lots of lip biting and tears for their love that can never be, although Julia would never dream of leaving Michael, even if she actually wanted to. And for Roger, her son, she plays the devoted mother, although he eventually sees through that, to the performance it really is. <br /><br />This is a great little melodrama, and a fast read. Maugham is the best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15832116120514111165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3164552240163761910">LOVED this book. It was such a delicious read. And Julia Lambert is a completely unforgettable character. I could have continued reading her story for another 1000 pages. The story follows the rise of a theatre (stage) star - Julia - and is told from her point of view and it's so fun to watch her 'perform' her way through life, through affairs, through motherhood, and watch (sometimes with shock, sometimes with awe) as she manipulates those around her, and the entire time you have this uneasy fe</span>
  <span id="freeText3164552240163761910" style="display:none">LOVED this book. It was such a delicious read. And Julia Lambert is a completely unforgettable character. I could have continued reading her story for another 1000 pages. The story follows the rise of a theatre (stage) star - Julia - and is told from her point of view and it's so fun to watch her 'perform' her way through life, through affairs, through motherhood, and watch (sometimes with shock, sometimes with awe) as she manipulates those around her, and the entire time you have this uneasy feeling that it is those around her who are the true manipulators. Is she so delusional as to truly believe that she is in charge of her life as she thinks? Or is she? I'm not sure. This book will leave you examining your own life and wondering which parts of it you act, and which parts are not a performance...truly this book was fascinating. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15963350086993793670">W. Somerset Maugham's, "Theatre," is a rich, insightful novel about the theatre and the people who work in the profession and the audiences that adore the actors. Maybe it's because I once was a theater major in college that I so thoroughly loved this novel. The character of Julia Lambert, a famous, extremely talent actress, who cannot disassociate everyday reality, from the characters she plays on stage is an amazing portrayal and depiction of the insecurities and obsessions that accompany grea</span>
  <span id="freeText15963350086993793670" style="display:none">W. Somerset Maugham's, "Theatre," is a rich, insightful novel about the theatre and the people who work in the profession and the audiences that adore the actors. Maybe it's because I once was a theater major in college that I so thoroughly loved this novel. The character of Julia Lambert, a famous, extremely talent actress, who cannot disassociate everyday reality, from the characters she plays on stage is an amazing portrayal and depiction of the insecurities and obsessions that accompany greatness. <br /><br />This book reminds me of the great movie "All About Eve" and like that movie this novel is as enthralling and mesmerizing as any book I have read about the theater. I strongly recommend this wonderful piece of art.  <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16679037028327785537">Very interesting work of Maugham’s. <br/>An amazing journey to the little theatrical world just before the cinema stole the attention of the audience from the theatre. History of the strong woman and very successful self-made actress. Her mind shown in smallest details by the great psychologist. How she loves herself, how she loves the image of her son created by her mind but not him, how she treated her husband, friends and fans. The world of the brilliant performer where nothing have real meaning a</span>
  <span id="freeText16679037028327785537" style="display:none">Very interesting work of Maugham’s. <br />An amazing journey to the little theatrical world just before the cinema stole the attention of the audience from the theatre. History of the strong woman and very successful self-made actress. Her mind shown in smallest details by the great psychologist. How she loves herself, how she loves the image of her son created by her mind but not him, how she treated her husband, friends and fans. The world of the brilliant performer where nothing have real meaning accept the fame and applause. Such brilliant acting that finally she couldn’t even separate her own identity from the characters she plays. She even speaks through them. Was there ever such a real person like Julia Lambert?<br />World around her – an empty headed men and women, pride, prejudice, actor’s jealousy. All that selfish people create a Court for the Queen. All except one – her own son.<br />Lie, false fillings, hypocrisy – essential entity of this society. But it’s not a dark world of despair (Maugham as always loyal to his own style) because the characters of the novel love it !<br />By the way – if you like to discuss anything which fascinates you or you want to share some thoughts of yours – you are always welcome ! My Skype : sempre_puerte. ( I’ve got a really boring job so don’t be shy 8- ).<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1561309909475246871"> <br/>I always love how Somerset Maugham tells a story, and his characters; and being a play write himself this subject matter is his area of expertise. <br/><br/>Here Maugham writes the story of Julia Lambert, a woman with so much talent she is one of the greatest actresses England–-she is so good, in fact, that perhaps she never stops acting.<br/><br/>Her teenage son tells her, “You see, I’ve lived all my life in an atmosphere of make-believe. … You never stop acting. It’s second nature to you. You act when there’s a </span>
  <span id="freeText1561309909475246871" style="display:none"> <br />I always love how Somerset Maugham tells a story, and his characters; and being a play write himself this subject matter is his area of expertise. <br /><br />Here Maugham writes the story of Julia Lambert, a woman with so much talent she is one of the greatest actresses England–-she is so good, in fact, that perhaps she never stops acting.<br /><br />Her teenage son tells her, “You see, I’ve lived all my life in an atmosphere of make-believe. … You never stop acting. It’s second nature to you. You act when there’s a party here. You act to the servants, you act to Father, you act to me. To me you act the part of the fond, indulgent, celebrated mother. You don’t exist, you’re only the innumerable parts you’ve played. I’ve often wondered if there was ever a you or if you were never anything more than a vehicle for all these other people that you’ve pretended to be. <br />When I’ve seen you go into an empty room I’ve sometimes wanted to open the door suddenly, but I’ve been afraid to in case I found nobody there.”<br /><br />This book will leave you examining your own life and wondering which parts of it you act, and which parts are not a performance…<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1561309909475246871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who would enjoy an easy and entertaining novel, and who has an interest in the theatre.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jean by: </span>
      I found the book in the library when I was 14 or 15. From then on I was hooked on the writing of Somerset Maugham.
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277493101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3144994938312944048">I read this book when I was a teenager, so although I remembered the main thrust of it, I didn't remember the finer details of the novel. Having read it so long ago, it is like a new story to me. I'm enjoying it very much as Somerset Maugham's style is straightforward and entertaining. I know very much more about the theatre now than I did when I was a schoolgirl, so this book is more meaningful to me now than it was all those years ago.<br/><br/>23 February<br/><br/>I finished reading the book recently and found i</span>
  <span id="freeText3144994938312944048" style="display:none">I read this book when I was a teenager, so although I remembered the main thrust of it, I didn't remember the finer details of the novel. Having read it so long ago, it is like a new story to me. I'm enjoying it very much as Somerset Maugham's style is straightforward and entertaining. I know very much more about the theatre now than I did when I was a schoolgirl, so this book is more meaningful to me now than it was all those years ago.<br /><br />23 February<br /><br />I finished reading the book recently and found it easy to read and extremely entertaining. Julia, the central character, was well-rounded. Her uncertain progress through an adulterous affair with a poor, unremarkable young man, who, at first, is over-awed and deferential towards her because of her exalted position in the theatre, is tortuous. Despite many twists and turns, Julia emerges from this experience a stronger and more fulfilled person.</span>

<div id="review_2279221437" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2279221437" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3548900-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277924024p2/3548900.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2279221437?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/3548900-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2279221437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14165309393052509944">Wonderful. <br/><br/>In 1930s England, famous theatre actress Julia Lambert has it all: a successful marriage and career, the love of the public, fame, fortune, you name it. Granted, the marriage is passionless, but they make a good team.<br/><br/>Enter a social climbing 23-year-old man who ignites a midlife crisis in Julia as the two embark upon an affair.<br/><br/>This is a fantastic book. Julia is an amazing character, and Maugham delivers his usual wit with a fascinating look at aging. I’m consistently amazed at Maugham’</span>
  <span id="freeText14165309393052509944" style="display:none">Wonderful. <br /><br />In 1930s England, famous theatre actress Julia Lambert has it all: a successful marriage and career, the love of the public, fame, fortune, you name it. Granted, the marriage is passionless, but they make a good team.<br /><br />Enter a social climbing 23-year-old man who ignites a midlife crisis in Julia as the two embark upon an affair.<br /><br />This is a fantastic book. Julia is an amazing character, and Maugham delivers his usual wit with a fascinating look at aging. I’m consistently amazed at Maugham’s ability to craft complicated, believable women. Just fabulous. Highly recommended.<br /><br />As for the film adaptation, <i>Being Julia</i>: I watched it years ago and enjoyed it. I rewatched it after finishing the novel, and it’s disappointing in comparison. The screenplay misses the underlying themes and rationales of the story and characters – including altering a key scene so that it undercuts Julia’s motives and genius – and the result is one more generic story about how actresses are such dramatic bitches. No thanks. So: Book, highly recommended; movie, skip. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14165309393052509944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1538297732" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1538297732" />
    <a title="Dr.J.G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52346722-dr-j-g"><img alt="Dr.J.G." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1538297732?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dr.J.G." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dr.J.G." href="/user/show/52346722-dr-j-g">Dr.J.G.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1538297732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer486618649868863219">Long ago, someone well rounded in education and interest in much of arts and other spheres, had once said reflectively, that of all the fine arts painting was comparatively most suitable for someone not interested in (or unable to) sell oneself; any performance art in giving one any success does take into account one's own looks, and while one might be extremely accomplished, if the audience does not like to see one that is the end of it, while anyone with good looks has his or her - especially </span>
  <span id="freeText486618649868863219" style="display:none">Long ago, someone well rounded in education and interest in much of arts and other spheres, had once said reflectively, that of all the fine arts painting was comparatively most suitable for someone not interested in (or unable to) sell oneself; any performance art in giving one any success does take into account one's own looks, and while one might be extremely accomplished, if the audience does not like to see one that is the end of it, while anyone with good looks has his or her - especially her - talents ignored or disparaged by those that envy and therefore pretend to disparage beauty. <br /><br />This is all the more so when age comes in, and even more for any woman, in most performing arts. As it is women in any field whatsoever, nowadays even in marriages in lands where divorces are easy and an everyday reality, tend to lose whatever interest their talent had generated and whatever level of respect they had earned due to their achievements, with age. Most judges are male or young, and respect for age, experience and wisdom is gone down during last century while the only criteria for respect seem to be looks apart from power of money or sheer physical power to hurt the other. Since women rarely are geared toward acquiring one, tend to lose if any of the other, and are seen merely as old even when extremely beautiful, age is seen as a tragedy without solution for women on the whole, with no roles and no regard for anyone over reproductive youth. <br /><br />Today moreover arts are marketed through other media than personal viewing, and the only winner in this respect is music where it is no longer quite entirely a performing art, what with radio, recording and distribution of recorded music having made watching and listening in person more a luxury post success rather than a necessity before success. <br /><br />But arts like dance and even acting do depend on looks, and it gets worse with age. Unless one has another sort of clout such as an underworld don approving of one and issuing orders to the contrary an actress or a dancer is pushed aside with age, and the bar is lowered all the time. In this respect thirty is the new eighty. Dance does give a performer another window in starting a school and training others, and thus imparting the skills perfected with age. Acting merely pushes an aged actress to do elderly roles and the bar is lowered on that where an actress playing a mother might in fact even be younger than her screen "son" in real life. <br />............................................................... <br /><br /><br />Maugham writes in this book about matters as they were in his time, a century ago. An actress with beauty and accomplishments and consequent fame, with a handsome theatre producer and director for a husband and a perfect son with a perfect life, and wonderful career due to very adoring audiences and long fame well into her forties, might in privacy realise she was not that young and be anxious about losing her youth, her popularity, her fame, her career, while it is still going strong and she is not yet history. And when an opportunity arrives to regain her youth in spirit - perhaps even in fact as a result of the affair - she takes it, and blooms with a return of youth and beauty. This is a story of such an affair and twists and turns. <br /><br />Julia has more than one adoring fan, and a long standing friend who has always waited on side with proposals over the years and has comfortably settled into a companionship of dinners and other harmless activities together, giving her a glow of not only a perfect home life but also being desired and seen publicly to be so desired. <br /><br />Now, at a moment of age creeping in, with producers etcetera looking at other - younger and newly established - actresses for the younger roles such as Juliet while making it clear she is intended to play Juliet's mother, she notices a very young man paying her attention, and in a moment of despondence with the loss of her popularity looming on horizon condescends to visit him in his lower middle class bachelor digs. It is as unexpected to her as to the reader when he makes advances without preamble or professing love and she - taken aback at this affront - ends up having sex for sheer lack of protest or defence. <br /><br />She is surprised, pleased at this proof of her still being attractive, and this continues until she finds him in bed with another, new and very young aspiring actress. He in fact asks her to help the new woman, unaware of her having found about the affair. And he is sullen when confronted with the relationship. <br /><br />Julia is in a quandary - if she says no, she might not only lose face in being seen as spiteful older woman but in fact might lose it all if the young man blabs about his having had sex with the older woman and her being interested while his dalliance was not serious. If she does help the younger woman, this is a sure step to her being sided to oblivion. <br /><br />How she deals with this, and to the surprise of reader succeeds completely, having made fools of everyone concerned and regained her supremacy in her position as the reigning diva, is the story told delightfully by Maugham as usual. <br /><br />Her art, her mastery of her art of acting, is half the reason for her being able to turn the tables and not only keep but regain all that is hers, to keep or discard as she would choose. Her sharp mind and connecting with her son is another factor, while her friend and his support being now discovered for its truth is yet another side story in this to bring a smile to the reader. <br /><br />Entirely delightful. </span>
  <a data-text-id="486618649868863219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_518286492" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/518286492" />
    <a title="Dorottya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15478679-dorottya"><img alt="Dorottya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569793742p2/15478679.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/518286492?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dorottya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dorottya" href="/user/show/15478679-dorottya">Dorottya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15478679-dorottya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer518286492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10924723796618249359">This book is absolutely fantastic. Let me tell you why.<br/><br/>First, it's about theatre. Okay, I am a bit obsessed with theatre... it's like, if a novel's about theatre, it starts with a point/star plus for me :). OK, just kidding, but I love reading about theatre and theatre customs of all eras. Plus, the edition I have has an owl pattern on the inner cover... I'm obsessed with owls, too. (and my boyfriend knows me really well since this gem was gifted to me by him :D)<br/><br/>Second, it has a really capturing</span>
  <span id="freeText10924723796618249359" style="display:none">This book is absolutely fantastic. Let me tell you why.<br /><br />First, it's about theatre. Okay, I am a bit obsessed with theatre... it's like, if a novel's about theatre, it starts with a point/star plus for me :). OK, just kidding, but I love reading about theatre and theatre customs of all eras. Plus, the edition I have has an owl pattern on the inner cover... I'm obsessed with owls, too. (and my boyfriend knows me really well since this gem was gifted to me by him :D)<br /><br />Second, it has a really capturing, nice-paced, exciting story. I couldn't put it down, it made itself read so much. Maugham made me interested in how this whole story would end.<br /><br />But honestly, what I loved this book the most for, were the characters. There are really few books like this, where the main character is not perfect (to be exact, she is full of flaws), yet we can feel for her, we can feel pity for her and we can understand her. I like how all the characters are not black and white in the story, except for those characters who we only see by Julia's eyes, but we really don't know them, just Julia's often rather silly theories about them.<br />Well, let's take a look at Julia. She's not an all good and innocent character. She's vain, she's vengeful, she can't be herself (just act, everywhere she goes), she's a hypocrite (she laughs at Tom for the same reason she got extremely angry at him), she's manipulative, she uses all the people around her, yet she expects everyone to adore her, she's rotten inside, yet she wants herself to be seen as virtuous, maybe with an attractive knack of flirting. However, we all know that it IS an effect of something. She is a woman, whose tragedy is that she doesn't know whom to love, how to love, or to truly love at all. She's chosen someone who she loved more than he loved her, and that kind of being left alone made her long after love and applause, which she got from her audience... and that led to that mayhem.<br />I had a feeling that the end of the book wasn't the end of her story and agony. It was just a moment of her life, the end of an era, a second when she felt happy. But I can't really think she could come to terms with her losing her sex appeal etc. In the last sentences, I felt the same kind of deceiving herself and acting a role... while being alone. This whole kind of self-deception and explaining people's behaviour to herself without trying to look into herself is still a common issue nowadays, too, I think, and it was grasped really authentically.<br /><br />I liked that Tom wasn't an all black, bad character. He was wonderfully written, too. Not only a cunning adulterer with prospects to get rich... just a hot-tempered young guy not aware of the meaning of true love, and slowly realizing the material sides and advantages of his connections.<br />I can tell the same about Michael's character. I hated him at first, as I saw him manipulative, heartless and empty, but it turns out that he actually has a good heart, it's just he's a bit vain and appearances matter more to him than emotions... yet that doesn't mean he's avil or doesn't care about anyone...</span>
  <a data-text-id="10924723796618249359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125135754" />
    <a title="Sandhya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya"><img alt="Sandhya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1244981990p2/215382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125135754?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandhya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandhya" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya">Sandhya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125135754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3537614966557456244">Somerset Maugham before he turned a full fledged novelist was an illustrious playwright. And even though he never got too involved in the workings of the theatre, was a close observer. It is some of this experience that eventually made its way into the novel he wrote later on in 1937.<br/>Theatre or/and its 2004 literary adaptation, Being Julia (directed by director István Szabó), is the story of an exquisitely talented and alluring stage actress Julia Lambert, and her trysts with various men. <br/><br/>So con</span>
  <span id="freeText3537614966557456244" style="display:none">Somerset Maugham before he turned a full fledged novelist was an illustrious playwright. And even though he never got too involved in the workings of the theatre, was a close observer. It is some of this experience that eventually made its way into the novel he wrote later on in 1937.<br />Theatre or/and its 2004 literary adaptation, Being Julia (directed by director István Szabó), is the story of an exquisitely talented and alluring stage actress Julia Lambert, and her trysts with various men. <br /><br />So consumed is Julia with her aura as an actress, and such is her versatility at playing different parts, that she never stops being a performer even when she is not on stage. As she embarks on a spectacular career, Julia gets enamoured by her good-looking and industrious co-actor, Michael. They are a happily married couple for a while, but soon Michael's vanity and business-minded approach to everything starts to bore Julia. She is a mega successful actress on the London stage, and by 40, she has all that an actress can possibly aspire for - plenty of money, a fleet of admirers, a husband who looks after her career interests, and a teenage son, whom she is content to see on and off.<br /><br />The rest:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://sandyi.blogspot.com/2010/10/somerset-maughams-theatre-and-being.html">http://sandyi.blogspot.com/2010/10/so...</a><br /></span>

<div id="review_884964732" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/884964732" />
    <a title="Gina Dalfonzo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5107430-gina-dalfonzo"><img alt="Gina Dalfonzo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498797950p2/5107430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/884964732?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gina Dalfonzo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gina Dalfonzo" href="/user/show/5107430-gina-dalfonzo">Gina Dalfonzo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer884964732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10938357048058056075">This book is essentially a character study of a very great, very selfish actress. It's insightfully and cleverly done -- so much so, that being in this woman's mind can be a rather suffocating experience! But it also offers great food for thought. Maugham has a trick of portraying a character negatively, and then slowly making you realize that the character isn't really so bad, it's just Julia's own warped way of seeing that person that's affecting the reader's perception. It's quite striking an</span>
  <span id="freeText10938357048058056075" style="display:none">This book is essentially a character study of a very great, very selfish actress. It's insightfully and cleverly done -- so much so, that being in this woman's mind can be a rather suffocating experience! But it also offers great food for thought. Maugham has a trick of portraying a character negatively, and then slowly making you realize that the character isn't really so bad, it's just Julia's own warped way of seeing that person that's affecting the reader's perception. It's quite striking and effective.<br /><br />A note about the ending . . . it fell a little flat for me. But most other reviewers seem to have liked it, so maybe that's just me being obtuse. :-)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10938357048058056075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2033995444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13370138490256445603">This may be one of Maugham's more forgettable stories, but it would be hard to forget Julia. Julia, who breezes through life as if it were a series of acts within a play. Who basks in flattering curtain calls, the good life, and the devotion of an almost-perfect husband. But most of the time, and perhaps I feel strongly about this in the absence of a comeuppance typical in a Maugham story, I just felt like throwing her a bunch of rotten tomatoes. <br/><br/>That said, ironically, she's one character I can </span>
  <span id="freeText13370138490256445603" style="display:none">This may be one of Maugham's more forgettable stories, but it would be hard to forget Julia. Julia, who breezes through life as if it were a series of acts within a play. Who basks in flattering curtain calls, the good life, and the devotion of an almost-perfect husband. But most of the time, and perhaps I feel strongly about this in the absence of a comeuppance typical in a Maugham story, I just felt like throwing her a bunch of rotten tomatoes. <br /><br />That said, ironically, she's one character I can very easily identify with. <br /></span>

<div id="review_185026090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185026090" />
    <a title="Paulina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2523408-paulina"><img alt="Paulina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595426282p2/2523408.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185026090?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paulina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paulina" href="/user/show/2523408-paulina">Paulina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185026090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5060775334743416936">Sometimes a bit overplayed, but oh so lovely. Maugham, the master of beautiful prose. And theatre. <br/>What could be better? All's fair in love and theatre. There are moments when you could kill Julia - the boy, I mean, the boy?! - but oh you fall in love with her and have a love-hate relationship. Not all writers manage to make the reader feel so much for the characters. And for this, Mr Maugham, I salute you. </span>

          </span>
        </div>
